Then release the stitch until it remains so that there is no rot on the limb, no abscess, no swelling, and similar things. If something happens from this (the treated area). Therefore, when the solution is applied to it, correct whatever has happened there completely, as we have said many times.
But if there is a splinter in the bone that it pierces: then it is necessary to equalize it, if it is possible for you. And if not, bind over it and remove it: and care about the one who preceded the reminder until it heals.
And at times this hip fracture is tightened, apart from the addition of pegs, as we said in aid and arm. (Translated from Latin)
However, in its restoration, such a fracture does not cause lameness to the weak. (Translated from Latin) And if it is restored alone, apart from applying another leg to it, then it is necessary that he who has it will always limp. (Translated from Latin)
Know that the hip becomes tightened in fifty days or less, or it is added too little, or it is reduced too little; all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions. (Translated from Latin)
Restoration of a fracture of the knee-cap. (Translated from Latin)
Know that the knee-cap rarely occurs a fracture, but it frequently occurs attrition. (Translated from Latin)
If indeed it occurs a fracture, then it is not other than a fissure or an opening in its parts. (Translated from Latin) It is that with a wound and without a wound. (Translated from Latin) It stands upon it entirely with feeling. (Translated from Latin)
Its restoration is as follows: equalizing what has been split from its parts with fingers until it is gathered and joined according to what is possible by equalization and ease, and construction. (Translated from Latin)
Then place a plaster made of rice flour or milled flour with egg white; and place upon it a round peg if needed; and bind above with a bandage suitable for that. (Translated from Latin)
Then consider all the dispositions which we have mentioned in other fractures; such as a hot abscess and similar cases; so that you present every occurrence with what is suitable for it until it heals. (Translated from Latin)
Restoration of a Leg Fracture.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one is large and called the shinbone (cruris); and the other is slender, and is called the calf bone (focile).
It happens with both kinds of fracture that it occurs with two bones of the arm; and because of this, its restoration is done equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones break simultaneously, the leg turns towards all directions.
If a bone breaks more finely, the leg turns towards the anterior part.
If a bone breaks coarsely, you will find it lower: and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer the extension, equalization, and ligature of stars equally according to that.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a dirty fracture with many setragies, it should have a smaller and softer extension, and a gentler alleviation by you.
And in the leg there is something about the operation that is similar to the arm: and it is, when you equalize the stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which are used in the making of solariums placed under tables, and they are made of spatulas of the palm; and choose those with a small thickness, and not too thin; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then fold one band over another with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side: let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two bands in three places at the two ends and in the middle. For it is forbidden to bind, the leg turns towards the right and left, and stands well with a good stance.
And now the cradle made of wood according to the length of the leg should be placed under it, to keep it from moving.
It is most important that you make this [the treatment] when there is a wound with the fracture itself.
Then consider the leg every day, and take care of it with utmost diligence to prevent aposteme, inflammation, or other things. If anything from those (afflicts), then oppose it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills it.
On the Fracture of Bones of the Foot and Fingers.
The calcaneum indeed does not suffer complete fracture.
The bones of the foot indeed sometimes suffer fracture, and fractures of the fingers do not happen very often: and they do not occur to them except by severe injury at several points.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs, and you see the bone protruding beyond itself, then place the sick man's foot on the ground prepared as when walking.
Then you stand there, and place your foot on that which is raised from those bones; then press or tread upon it; and hold it equally, until it returns to its places.
Then place a poultice and a plaster on top of it; and put a small table beneath the foot with two flat heads.
Then bind it with a decent bandage when you have wrapped it in linen and other things that are needed.
When three or four days have passed, then unbind the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned, whether they are broken or displaced. The operation is therefore as you see.
If some fingers are broken, then restore them, and align them as I have told you in the restoration of fingers of the hand. Then place a stylus along the length of your finger and let it be slightly wider than the finger; then put this table beneath the foot as I have told you, and bind it with a decent bandage.
If what is broken is two or three bones, or more, then place a stylus of the same size over each finger and wrap it in linen; and bind the table beneath the foot; and let there be a standing on one head of the table outside the foot to hold it with good retention.
It is fitting for you, lest you forget, to consider all that I told you about the other fractures regarding the accidents we mentioned; and apply the appropriate solution to every accident.
On the fracture of a woman's jaw, and the breastbone, and a man's long bone.
When a woman's jaw is fractured, then make her sit in a square position. Then lean her towards the back of the same slightly and hold her in that position.
Next, a midwife fills the woman's jaw with a linen cloth until it is full; and the jaw becomes as round as a sphere from this.
Then strike the woman gently, and gradually reduce her back. This linen cloth emerges until it becomes before the mouth of the jaw as round as a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a small cushion under her back. Whenever she wishes to spit, let her easily remove the linen cloth, then spit; and bring it back according to the amount you first inserted; then return to the cushion again.
Let this be done for seven days or approximately; for the bone itself recovers.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a hen, and tie it around its orifice with a reed tube, and insert the entire bladder into her jaw. Then blow through the tube with force until the bladder is inflated inside the jaw; for the fracture returns.
Then it should be filled with linen cloth and stay for some days as we have said, until it heals.
When the breastbone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer its treatment and apply what we have said regarding the anchorage for the bone.
And it is not hidden that the right [side] is not concealed in close fractures which happen little, particularly for one who has some interest or practice.
Understand my book very much, because the fracture itself indicates to you in many dispositions the way of the right [side] in its restoration and the narrowing of it: therefore understand.
When a man's member breaks, take the throat of a goose; and insert the staff [into it]. Then place upon it a covering from cloth, and wrap [it], and let it be for three days or about them, and it will heal if God wishes.
On the restoration of bone fracture when there is a wound.
It is appropriate for the one who has a fracture along with a wound, especially if it is large like the hip bone or humerus, to rush and summon a phlebotomist immediately; if you are informed of the conditions of phlebotomy as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary for you to rush for its amputation, so that you pour powdered lime over it if nothing else is present for you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day; and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
If however, an abscess hot occurs, then delay the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days, for you will cause him to suffer additional harm.
If the broken bone is sticking out from the skin exposed: then it is necessary to try to bring it back and align it with your hands easily and slightly extended.
If however the extension is not prepared, and the arm itself is equal to your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And the tool is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight fingers: and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for that reason it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity required for the treatment of any kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let its thickness be small, so as not to increase during the application of pressure on it during the hour of surgery: and let its sharp edges have a margin at their ends. And let the upper part of it be thick up to the middle, but below that let it be thin.
And this is its shape.
In this operation, the sharp end should be bent and placed over the protruding bone's extremity: and push it with it together until when the bone returns and is equalized in some way.
Therefore try to align the fracture ends against each other.
If the fracture ends are fine and do not accept the tool properly, then it is necessary to cut the bone end until the tool can hold it.
If however you cannot reduce the bones with what I have described, then cut it with the chisels we mentioned, or saw it with one of your saws in any way possible for you.
Then plane down what remains on the bone from roughness and fine layers.
When the bone is reduced and after its removal a very strong pain arises that hurts it, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
Make it so. For you bring great help to the sick, when you complete the restoration that you do for the bone.
Then immerse the linen in strong wine, black. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place on the wound cicatricles or anything containing oil; so that it does not cause decay and corruption at the same spot.
Then administer the splinters in the hour of the completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound undressed; thus, so that you cut with forceps a hole in the swellings according to the size of the wound.
Be careful not to squeeze the wound with the fracture. For often have fools done this; and they have caused death, or corruption, or gangrene on their own patients, that is, pus. And your suture be soft, gentle, diverse from the other sutures for fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some bad incidents that we have mentioned, and you find pain at a suspended place; then it is not necessary to place splinters on it. And make swellings from hard cloths in the place of splinters and stitch it with them.
And when it has been a day or two, and you see the wound begin to produce pus; then remove from above it the linen that you had placed on it with wine. Then administer linen and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds; such as ointment of four ingredients (tetrapharmacon) and similar ones.
And it is suitable for you to dissolve the suture. And consider the wound every day morning and evening until it heals and cures.
It is necessary to prepare the limb in such a way that the pus runs out from it with ease towards the lower parts.
If many days pass and the wound does not heal nor the pus is discharged from it: then you know that there are small fractures of bones in that place. It is necessary to examine the wound with pressure (or probing). As for how many of those small fractures have been separated, remove them and extract them. And those which have not been separated and are boring the limb and causing pain, try to cut them and remove them in every way possible for you.
If pimples (zuchen) or boils (ekileten), i.e., abscesses, or any other corruption and putrefaction should occur at the wound: then it is necessary to apply each of them with what corresponds to it from the remedies whose narration preceded in their respective chapters.
And from that where my discourse must remain silent, and to whom you should direct your mind when a bone breaks and protrudes upon the limb, such as the hip bone, or the shoulder, or similar large bones, it is not to presume its attraction; for often death occurs from it, indeed let it be, until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will fall by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Therefore treat the wound if it seems to you; and if not, let it be.
Chapter on the treatment of a nodule (Nodation) in the fracture of a limb.
This nodule occurs many times in the course of the healing of a fracture. Above all, that which is near joints; and it becomes foul from this figure of the limb. And perhaps it hinders the limb from its natural movement.
Therefore consider if the nodule is recent; administer to it remedies that have astringent properties, such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, gum Arabic, acacia and similar ones.
Therefore, take some or all of them [the medicines]: and bind them with staunchion wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the swelling, and tightly bind them over the good constriction.
And release the binding, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another one until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a sheet of lead well. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
If the swelling has already turned into a stone and is tightly bound and there is an urgent need to remove it: then bind something above it from its upper part, and cut off the excessive protrusion; or shave it with certain scrapers until it is removed; and take care of it until it heals.
[Chapter title: Treatment when the member has been restored but remains tender after that]
When a bone fracture is restored and the member remains tender after that, weak: then it is not done except for many reasons. One of the reasons is a multiplicity of loosening and tying of the ligament not as required; or due to an excessive binding of the ligaments, preventing nutrition from reaching the member; or due to an excess of embrocation at an inappropriate time; or due to a lack of blood in the body of the sick person and its weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the sick person and make his body soft until blood increases in him. And apply a bath. And pour joy and happiness over him.
Then place pitch on the member: so that it attracts much nourishment to itself. Apply the ointment to it with warm water until the wound heals and returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are set twistedly.
When a restored member, after its healing, becomes twisted or protrudes due to fractured bone or nodulation, and is disfigured by this condition of the limb; however, the limb does not prohibit its natural operation; then it is not necessary that you accept the advice of him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and restorers, who did this in our land.
This operation is extremely blameworthy, leading to death or great fear, lest it be fatal.
But if lameness and nodulation are recent: then it is necessary to apply warm water in which soothing herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves of althea, its root, melilotus, and similar ones. And make for him an ointment with a soothing plaster, such as well-made dyachilon.
Or take the mucilage from the root of althea, pound it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and apply it with that.
Or figs are taken, powdered with dove dung, and similar substances that are called from medicines reducing consolidation.
And sometimes nodulation is resolved through persistent gentle rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And the movement of the limb should be administered to all its parts every hour.
If the twisting has already occurred, and it has become strong, and has turned into a stone, and its treatment with iron is necessary: then it is necessary to make an incision above it, and to break the continuation of the bone, and to cut off what is superfluous from nodulation or bone with fine instruments; and apply ease to that with care and diligence.

I'm not a real person but am The dislocation is to be reduced by means of a complete reduction until it becomes softened at the chest part, until the salve for the weak has run and it cannot hold it, nor can it close its own mouth, and its tongue is wrapped with speech.
When the dislocation is slight, then by itself and with few positions it returns.
If the dislocation is complete and entire: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and it should not be delayed too much; and it is to be such that the attendant holds the head of the weak person; and the doctor is to insert his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated mandible between the bones if the dislocation is from a single part. Or he is to insert two thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from two parts. And the other fingers of his hands are to be outside, which equal it.
And command the weak person to cleanse his jaws and to purify them for eating entirely. The doctor holds the jaw equal, and he pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together: then administer steaming with hot water and oil until it becomes easy to reduce both; nor should you delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When they return and equalize, and the mouth of the weak person is closed, and they do not soften: then place on them poultices of cloth, with a mixture made of wax, and rose oil.
Then it is tied with soft bands.
And let the sleep of the weak person be on his back; and let his head rest between two poultices, so that he does not move it to the right and left; nor should he try or be forced to chew something; instead let his food be made soft, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is relaxed: then he may eat what he wants, and let that be given with ease.
Do not let him eat or drink at the operation on his mouth, and do not let him speak, until the mandible is sutured and healed.
If the reduction of the two jawbones becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places: then often fevers occur, and constant vomiting happens; and perhaps the weakened stomach dissolves, and possibly it vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see that: then you know that it is a loss, and he who experiences this usually dies within ten days.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocations of the neck (jaw) and the end of the arm.
The jaw is not dislocated from the inside out, because of its continuity with the chest. It is dislocated to the outside sometimes and this is made clear to the senses.
The restoration of it (the jaw) is that he lies weak on his back and extends his arms. Then make room with your compression, for it shall return itself.
Place upon it an ointment and a powder, then bind it.
However, the parts that follow the elbow should not be dislocated, unless rarely. If it is dislocated on any day: it must be reduced and set according to what was said, and that which has been prepared for you.
Then place upon it an ointment, a powder, and a bandage. And command the sick man to adhere to calmness and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the humerus should be reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Chapter on the Reduction of Dislocations of the Humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One way is when it dislocates towards the region of the pectoral girdle below. The second way is when it dislocates to the chest region. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humero (shoulder): and that occurs rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, due to the spoon-shaped process (of the humerus). And it is not dislocated to the front, due to the nerves.
And indeed it is mostly dislocated and exits towards the lower part turned like a spindle; particularly in those whose flesh is scanty. Since it exits rapidly in them, and enters rapidly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, that is, since it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow or accident, and the humerus is dislocated with heat, and it is thought because of that, since it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, one must test that, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
The dislocation is known when it turns towards the lower part like a spindle; and you will distinguish between the dislocated humerus and the healthy one. For you will find among them a clear difference, and in the head of the humerus a hollow, and beneath the spindle-like part on touch of the head of the humerus as if it were an egg; and an incapacitated person cannot bring the hand to the ear nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards; then you will find clear evidence of touch and it will not be hidden.
And this dislocation is easy to reduce when it is recent or the child is weak.
The reduction of it (the dislocation) is that the attendant raises his hand towards the upper parts. Then place your thumbs beneath the spindle part of it and lift the joint with force to its place; and the attendant raises his hand and extends it upward. Then place her below, for he himself returns quickly.
If he does not return within what I have said, and there is from the beginning the dislocation of joints for several days: then it is necessary that the sick person be bathed in hot water, and given a poultice which softens and soothes, such as if they decoct the roots of althea, and fenugreek, and trefoil in water, and give it.
Then the sick person shall lie on his back upon his back; and let there be placed beneath him a cushion, and woolen cloth which is equal in tension between softening and binding.
Then the physician shall place his heel upon the sphere, and shall lift the head of the humerus with force, and draw the hand of the sick person to below.
And if you wish, return him in this manner: and it is for you to make a tall man present who is taller than the sick person, and let him stand by his side, and insert his arm beneath the sick person's cushion; and lift the cushion upwards until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another assistant shall draw the hand of the sick person to below his abdomen. If the sick person is light: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also returned in another manner: and it is for you to fashion on the ground a long stick, whose head is round in shape, such as a mitten-handle; not too thick, nor too thin. Then let there be placed beneath the sick person after the sticks are placed on his head, soft cloths; and the sick person shall stand along the length of the stick.
Then extend his hand to the lower part on one side, and extend his body from the other side with strength: for the connection returns to its place quickly.
If his reduction is difficult by everything that has been said, then use this care: and it is, that you take a stick whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: to which head should be round, that it softens the entrance into the deep place by rubbing, according to this form.
Then wrap soft cloth around the round head, so as not to hurt the weak stick.
Then place it under the weak rubbing (place), so that his hand or arm is extended over the stick to the lower part: and bind the stick above the assistance, and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then place his arm on a step of the ladder by its width, and extend his hand to the lower part, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. For the connection enters immediately.
When his entrance is completed according to whatever way it is possible: then it is necessary, that you place under the weak rubbing (place) a sphere equal in quantity from wool. Then place the prepared plaster from milled flour with balm and egg white on his shoulder, just as it is turned over.
Then tie under the weak rubbing (place) a suitable stitch: and turn the bandage over the plaster above: and connect his hand to his neck, and let it not be moved, nor move his hand for seven days.
It is necessary, that food be given to the sick in small quantity, until he strengthens the limb: because it is quicker for its healing.
Then let it be placed wider after seven days or five, and rub gently with movement. If therefore it becomes firm and does not become soft, then it is already healed.
If the joint is dislocated several times, due to the moisture that occurs to it, or due to another cause: then it is necessary, that a cautery be administered to it with three holes, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
If this is done and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not become firm, and the plaster and constriction are applied on it several times, and it does not become firm and falls, and cannot be lifted up: then you know, that the nerves which are towards the head of the humerus have been cut or extended or softened: and you know, that the joint will never adhere in its place.
The dislocation that is towards the chest and breast, or to the back, is reduced by pressure and extension with hands: until it returns, and care is administered to it afterwards, until it is healed.
If after healing a pain occurs in the limb, and sluggishness in its movement: then the weak should use baths many times, until that pain is relieved, and it returns to its first nature.
Chapter on the treatment of shoulder dislocation.
Know that the shoulder joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly it returns with difficulty too; and indeed it is dislocated to all parts, and particularly to the front or back.
And its dislocation will not be hidden from you: because it falls under your sight, and under touch, according to whatever shape it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated shoulder with a healthy one, it will be made manifest to you. The joint should be hollowed out, and the arm cannot be doubled nor touch with it its own humerus.
It is necessary that you hasten and reduce dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
If a hot abscess occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it does not heal altogether, especially when it is in the back region. For all kinds of dislocations, it is worse; and it brings a more violent pain, and death often descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of the patient should be extended with two hands; and the arm should be expanded, and the hands of the physician should be above and below the elbow, and he himself should push the disjoint joint with the tips of his fingers or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is towards the front part: then it returns in such a way that he separates his hand with his hand until he strikes with the root of his palm on the elbow that is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey in reduction, then administer a strong and vigorous traction; and it is that two assistants extend the arm; and two assistants hold him also, so that he is not moved during the traction.
Then turn the arm to every part after it has been wrapped around his hand with a double cloth or broad bandage.
And when the physician touches the disjoint joint with his hand, he binds it with oil; so that it is one of those things that help in lubricating the disjointure with ease. Then push the disjoint joint with a vigorous push until it returns.
After its reduction, it is necessary to place a plaster on it that has astringency and drying with ox-gall; and it should be sewn up with a suitable suture. And let the arm of the sick person be suspended from his neck; and let it remain for some days.
Then, it shall be loosened. If the joint has become fixed at its location, then undo the binding from it, and let it (the arm) be.
And if you see the joint not tightly bound, then repeat the plaster, and bind again, and let it (the arm) be for some days until it becomes tightly bound. Then, undo it (the arm).
If pain occurs after the reduction of the joint and it has movement: then administer humectation in bathing, and fine friction, and compression until it (the pain) settles, or place on the joint an ointment of the fat of the ram's wool. Then, let it be bound; and let it (the arm) remain on it by day and night. Then, remove it (the ointment), and immerse him into the bath. When he sweats, then frict the joint with a moderate friction. Then, apply to it (the joint) three times with ointment, first, second, and third, when entering the bath, until it becomes soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it (the joint) horse dung, moistened and heated with butter, and bind over it.
Do this (the treatment) at times. It becomes soothed, and returns to its proper position.
Chapter on the cure of dislocations by Albucasis.
Mahassem often dislocates his hand, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy. On the contrary, other joints (are difficult to reduce). However, it is necessary that speed should be in the reduction of this dislocation within an hour from when it was dislocated, before the place is opened; or a warm abscess may occur at the site.
Reduction of dislocation is to place Mahassem's weak hand on a table, and let his attendant extend it; and the physician shall place his palm upon the prominence of the joint, and push until it returns.
However, it should be considered if the palm has already retreated towards the back of the hand; then let the weak person place the back of his hand on the table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwardly upon the table, that is, the inner part of his hand shall be placed upon the table, so that the physician falls upon the same joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with plaster sitting on an abscess; and let him be, do not redo it.
For if it cannot bear [the treatment], and you cannot do reduction of it after the days have passed, but still the joint remains according to its twist; and nothing harms the weak person except what softens the hand, and nothing can be bound; then know that the nerves are cut off or rubbed.
Therefore, there is no skill in it, except to bind with a cautery near its loss. And perhaps it does not bring anything about.
When Almahassem does not return, then place upon him the plaster that I have mentioned. Then bind it and let it be for five days; afterwards, it is to be loosened and let it be, and the hand should be exercised.
If it loses movement and something heavy happens to it; then soften it with warm water, and rub it softly several times, until it becomes soft.
[Paragraph on the treatment of finger dislocations.]
When a finger is dislocated from any part of it, or the finger to the back of its palm, or its back; then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then hold the finger's head and suspend it towards the side where it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
Then extend it until it becomes straight on its own day.
When it is night, bind it according to the story told, and do not stop unbinding it during the day; exercise it with movement and bind it at night.
Do that for several days until it is tightened.
And do the same with it if it is dislocated towards the stomach of the hand; bind it to that side and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And do the same with it when it is dislocated towards other parts.
Chapter on the treatment of spondylolisthesis of the back.
When spondylolisthesis of the back or neck occurs, or when many vertebrae are moved from their places; then there is no treatment for it because death approaches the weak.
And the sign of this is that hunger leaves the weak without will and cannot hold it; the limbs become soft several times from him, or two legs or two arms of himself, or one of them; or a vertebra is moved from its place.
For it is often moved because the removal occurs to four directions.
Therefore, that which is moved towards the back is called humpback.
But the treatment of it is that you should consider if humpback occurs from childhood; then there is no treatment for it and no cure at all.
But in that which occurs from an accident, blow, or similar things, the ancient methods of treatment have already been said in a lengthy speech. Of which much does not return with profit.
I have already shortened it from that one, whose little justifies its much due to my proximity to the intention; and in my exposition of it, I have shaped the instrument differently from how they presented and explained it.
Therefore, I say that when there is a hump occurring on the anterior part, it is not a natural state, nor can healing come from it.
And similarly, in that which occurs to both sides as well.
And it is not cured except for that which occurs in the back itself, as I have said; and it is so that the weak person is extended over his face on the bench, nearly up against the wall; and a damp cloth is spread beneath him to avoid hurting his chest.
Then place a straight piece of wood fixed in the fireplace on the ground at the foot of the bed, about his head; and another piece of wood about his feet at the end of the other bed; and let a servant hold the wood, and it should not be fastened to the fireplace. And the second servant holds the other.
Then cover the head of the weak person with a soft cloth, and tie a firm bandage around his neck under his chin; and extend the end of the bandage to the wood near his head, and tie it there.
Then tie another bandage above his ankles and above his knees, and near his fetters. Then gather all the bands together and tie them to the other wood near his feet.
Then all the servants extend the band to the part of the wood and they must not move the two woods from their places where they are fixed; however, they should not be fastened as we said.
And the physician shall place his hands on the thigh with strength until he returns; or he may place a table over it. Then he attaches his feet to the table until he returns.
If he does not return through this treatment, then take a table whose length is about three arms. And bore a hole in the wall near the sick man, where the end of the table may enter. Then place the middle of the table on the hip bone (Spondyle). And the physician places his feet at the other end (of the table). And bind it tightly until the hip bone is dislocated and he returns to his place.
Then, if you wish, operate with a drill that is turned by hand. It is so that you mark on the ground near the head of the sick man two pieces of wood, whose length is one arm each; and at his head near the last foot, two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made everywhere where the drill runs through, and each piece of wood is fixed firmly to the ground so that it does not move at all.
A round piece of wood is inserted; and there is a drill inside, where the ligament is turned through two holes in the two pieces of wood.
And at the end of it (the drill) there is a hole, where the piece of wood is fixed, whose length is one palm with what is turned; and similarly in the other two pieces of wood.
Then bind the ligaments that you tied around the chest of the sick man on the drill near his head; and the ligaments that you tied around his thighs on the drill near his feet.
Then the assistant stands near every drill, who turns his hand with a cloth (linen), with which the drill is turned; and the physician aligns the hip bone as we have said.
This is the form of a hernia, which follows.
Then when the fracture (Spondilis) returns and the place is leveled, then it is necessary that you apply a dry plaster with egg white. Then a wick of linen or wool should be inserted into the wound from the plaster and on top of it place a wooden splint whose width is that of three fingers or about that. And its length should be such that it encloses the swelling and over the healthy part of the fracture.
Then it should be bound with the cord as previously said. And the sick person should take a subtle food until he is healed.
If something remains projecting in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines be given which soften and soothe, with the use of the tablet as previously said for a long time. And now lead is applied to it.
Occasionally a projecting swelling arises in the last fracture of the back; it is supposed to be dislocation and that bone which is now projecting. Therefore, it is not advisable to make a presumption about this medicine; perhaps it will cause death.
Chapter on the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
You should know that the joints of the ankle and shoulder do not occur except by dislocation; nor does it happen to them as it happens to the other joints from a slight separation and hollowing out.
The joint of the ankle is dislocated in four parts. Because it is dislocated forward and backward.\]
The most frequently dislocated are those that are internal: few are dislocated towards the front and back.
The treatment for dislocation of this kind towards the internal is as follows: because when you join the weak leg with the healthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the weak one projects more than that of the healthy, and the weak cannot double its foot at the inguinal region; there is a place following the inguinal region that is indented by an indentation made with heat, because the head of the hip has already reached that location.
The sign of a dislocation occurring towards the external is as follows: because its signs are opposite to these signs.
The sign of a dislocation occurring towards the front is as follows: because it extends the healthy leg beyond its normal length; however, it does not double it without pain in the knee; and if it attempts to walk, it cannot move forward over that leg; and urine retention occurs in it, and the inguinal region of it should be massaged, and at walking there is a bruising of it on the heel.
The sign of a dislocation occurring towards the back is as follows: it does not extend the knee, nor can it double it before doubling the inguinal region; and the leg is even shorter than the other; and the inguinal region is softened, and the head of the hip is at that location.
The reduction of this dislocation, however, is as follows: that you consider then whether the dislocation is old and has occurred with someone who already has it. Then if you apply the reduction to it, and it remains in its original position, then there is no treatment at all in that case. Therefore, it is not appropriate for a presumption to be made regarding this.
In the case of someone whose dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocations: then go to this, that you wish to join the bones together, and extend them towards the internal and external, and move it left and right; perhaps it will return, and no other care will be needed.
If it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part; either with his hands, or with a ligature that can be tied above his knee: and another assistant should extend his arm above, so that he may insert his hand beneath his armpits. Then tie a soft ligature over the root of the hip and hold the end of another ligament.
And let its extension be either from the front of the groin to the chest area, or from the back to the back area.
And let all the extensions be the same until the sick man, along with his body, is raised from the ground and remains hanging.
For this is indeed a common type of extension among the four types.
If the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to do what I will say about its own treatment.
In reducing it for itself when the dislocation is to the inside, it is necessary for the sick man to lie on his healthy side. Then place a ligature over the root of the hip, in the area that is between the head of the hip and the area under the groin. Then extend the ligature upward from the groin to the upper part of the body, to the chest area.
Then another strong assistant takes his arms, and he reduces the large area of the weak hip and extends it with a powerful extension to the outside. Now it returns to its place.
And this is an easier treatment than the other treatments for reducing this limb.
If it is dislocated above you and does not obey you with this type of treatment, then it is necessary to tie the feet of the sick man together with a strong ligature, a soft one, over both heels and both knees: and let each length be four finger widths in comparison to its own; and let the sick leg be extended more than the other two fingers.
Then the sick person is suspended over the head of a beam, which is in the house; and its length from the ground should be the measure of two arms.
Then command a strong youth to brace the head and loins; another youth shall suspend them with the sick person; and another shall push, bracing the loins with strength, for the joining returns to its place swiftly.
In his own reduction, when it is a dislocation towards the outer parts, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a platform, as we have said in one who has a hump. And tie a bandage over his foot and over his chest, for the sick person himself.
Then two beams are placed, one at his feet and another at his head. Then place a beam in the middle of the platform, fixed firmly; over which they should be wrapped with wet cloths so as not to hurt the sick person; and let there be a beam between his loins so that he is not drawn downwards during stretching.
Then let every attendant go to his place, and the physician shall supervise the reduction with his hand.
If therefore he obeys in returning, it will be good.
And if not, place him on the platform and press it equally, as we have said for one who has a hump. Nevertheless, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the anterior parts, then it is necessary to extend the sick person's leg quickly; and this same story applies on the platform; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the loins of the sick person. Then bend him with one hand, and with this bending place him extended to the lower parts towards the knee.
If the dislocation is towards the posterior parts, then it is not necessary to extend the sick person downwards and lift him off the ground; instead, he should be placed on a hard surface, as it is also necessary when his ankle is dislocated towards the outer parts, according to what we said about his lying on two surfaces: one on his face and his legs extended: according to what we said recently.
It is necessary that a compression be applied with a flat object on the joint where it exits, when reduction is completed as required.
And the sign of reduction is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend two legs of the weak: and when you see them equal, and the weak person pulls his own leg, and expands it without displacement: then you will know that the limb has returned as required: then join the hips, and place the plaster, and bind with bandages, that is, a roller: thus, so that it does not move out of place to any part: and let the weak person remain quiet for three or four days.
Then undo the bandage, and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then you will know that the dislocation is already fixed: therefore release the weak person to walk.
And if you see something softening: then return and apply a plaster, and bind it as per your first binding: and leave it for three days as well.
Then undo that and make it slow in walking over some days, until it recovers well.
Chapter on Dislocations of the Knee.
The knee can be dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated towards the outside, towards the inside, and towards the bottom (i.e., the back).
And it is not dislocated towards the front.
And the sign of dislocation is, that you order the weak person to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not stick with the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of dislocation is, that you make the weak person sit straight, so that he can now extend his leg if possible over it, and let his back touch your face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his leg adhere with your palms, and place them straight upon his leg between your fingers. Then apply both sides of the leg with the palm of your hand with strength. And let another servant extend his foot until the leg returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the foot will adhere with the thigh in a gentle manner, not forcefully.
Then apply ointment to it, and make his foot adhere with the thigh. Then bind them together with linen cloth for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And the sick man shall not walk for some days until he is healed.
If the reduction is refused to you, then administer a strong traction with ropes, whose method of application for the ankle has been previously described in the treatment section, until it returns.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes displaced slightly and sometimes fully dislocated according to its extent. Its dislocation may be towards the inside or the outside.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and projecting towards the direction to which it has been dislocated.
The cure for its removal is easy reduction: and it is to extend it gently with the hands and align it until it returns.
In the cure of its full dislocation, it is necessary that you make the sick man sit upright and hold him firmly from behind his back, in the middle of himself.
Then hold his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand below the foot at the place of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards yourself with your right hand. Then with two-thirds [of the limb].
Then extend with left hand and push the sole of the foot with right hand turned toward the leg without strain.
Do that twice, as we have said.
Then push the sole of the foot to the leg one-third of its length and you pull the ligament.
If it returns to its original position in one or two ways according to this method and you see the foot even, it is good; and if not, then perform the operation again on it; because it will return.
If the reduction is prohibited to you with it as we have said; then make the weak person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a small peg firmly in the ground so that he falls between his hips; and now roll a cloth over him, so that he does not injure the weak one.
Then take hold of his hip with a servant; afterwards stretch out the servant the leg, or with his hands or with the strap that binds over the ankle. Then all servants should stretch opposite to their companion's extension; and the peg should stand between the weak one's hips holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and another servant holds the healthy leg downwards. Because dislocation returns quickly.
When the dislocation returns and the health of the reduction is declared to you; then apply ointment and plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot with a strap to the leg.
And it is necessary that you be careful or afraid of the nerves that are above the heel at the back, so that the bandage is not too tight and injures them.
Then let him go for two or three days. If the ligament is softened, tighten it; then dissolve it on the third or fourth day, and prevent the weak from walking for forty days. If it attempts to walk before that period, there is no certainty but that it will be loosened again due to the dislocation, corrupted, and not receive subsequent treatment after that.
If it happens to him an abscess that is warm: then it is necessary for you to administer for its sedation what was mentioned earlier in our narrative at another place concerning the treatment, and embrocation until it is removed.
Chapter on Dislocation of Fingers of the Foot, and Cure.
It is necessary that it be reduced as soon as possible without exertion from those who have been dislocated with slight extension, and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If the dislocation is in some of the knots of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the weak person sit with his foot on a level surface with the ground or on a table, and he stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns and you see it leveled again, and no projection appears in its place.
Then put a table under the belly of his foot which can hold his whole foot, having two heads. Then bind it with a bandage for three days. Then dissolve it, and let him rest from walking for many days until it is bound up and secured after the reduction.
Chapter on Types of Dislocations with Wound or Fracture.
When something happens to him and you attempt the treatment and restoration of that, death often occurs repeatedly; and therefore it is not advisable to presume on his cure, which is such, unless he who does this is experienced in long practice, merciful, devout, patient, cautious, not daring, nor presumptuous: and that he should use at the beginning of the treatment medications that soothe abscesses hot only: and let him be entrusted to divine judgment. If thou seest, or hopest for salvation from destruction with mildness of sickness, and where something of hope appears. Endeavor therefore the reduction of it immediately at the beginning, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the member returns, as we wish, then administer a regime that suppresses abscess: and care for the wound with it what suits it from drying ointments.
If there is dislocation with fracture, and separated sores occur in the bone, then endeavor to remove them, and make as I have said in simple diseases at the sites of those.
And be careful according to your worth, and guard yourself from entering into the path of fear: according to what has gone before my speech to you.
For that is better for thee with thy Creator, and more lasting for thy glory, and more safe for thy body with them at thy time.
